<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent contends that there are no white panelists similarly situated to Fields and to panel member Joe Warren because "&nbsp;'&nbsp;"[s]imilarly situated" does not mean matching any one of several reasons the prosecution gave for striking a potential juror--it means matching <em>all</em> of them.'&nbsp;"  <em>Post</em>, at 19 (quoting <em>Miller-El</em> v. <em>Cockrell,</em> 537 U.&nbsp;S., at 362-363 (<em>Thomas, J., </em>dissenting)).  None of our cases announces a rule that no comparison is probative unless the situation of the individuals compared is identical in all respects, and there is no reason to accept one.  Nothing in the combination of Fields's statements about rehabilitation and his brother's history discredits our grounds for inferring that these purported reasons were pretextual.  A <em>per se</em> rule that a defendant cannot win a <em>Batson</em> claim unless there is an exactly identical white juror would leave <em>Batson</em> inoperable; potential jurors are not products of a set of cookie cutters.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Each of them was black and each was peremptorily struck by the State after Woods's acceptance.  It is unclear whether the prosecutors knew they were black prior to the <em>voir dire</em> questioning on the stand, though there is some indication that they did: prosecutors noted the race of each panelist on all of the juror cards, <em>Miller-El</em> v. <em>Cockrell</em>, 537 U.&nbsp;S., at 347, even for those panelists who were never questioned individually because the week ended before it was their turn.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Nor is pretextual indication mitigated by Macaluso's further reason that Warren had a brother-in-law convicted of a crime having to do with food stamps for which he had to make restitution.  App. 910.  Macaluso never questioned Warren about his errant relative at all; as with Fields's brother, the failure to ask undermines the persuasiveness of the claimed concern.  And Warren's brother's criminal history was comparable to those of relatives of other panel members not struck by prosecutors.  Cheryl Davis's husband had been convicted of theft and received seven years' probation.  <em>Id.,</em> at 695-696.  Chatta Nix's brother was involved in white-collar fraud.  <em>Id.</em>, at 613-614.  Noad Vickery's sister served time in a penitentiary several decades ago.  <em>Id.</em>, at 240-241.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;But even if Macaluso actually had explained that he exercised the strike because Warren was diffident about imposing death, it would have been hard to square that explanation with the prosecution's tolerance for a number of ambivalent white panel members.  Juror Marie Mazza, for example, admitted some concern about what her associates might think of her if she sat on a jury that called for the death penalty.  <em>Id.</em>, at 354-355.  Ronald Salsini, accepted by the prosecution but then struck by the defense, worried that if he gave the death penalty he might have a "problem" in the future with having done so.  <em>Id.</em>, at 593.  Witt, another panel member accepted by the State but struck by the defense, said she did not know if she could give that sentence.  6 Record 2423.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court of Appeals also found ambivalence in Warren's statement, when asked how he felt generally about the death penalty, that, "there are some cases where I would agree, you know, and there are others that I don't."  App. 202.2 (quoted in 361 F.&nbsp;3d 849, 857 (CA5 2004)).  But a look at Warren's next answers shows what he meant.  The sorts of cases where he would impose it were those where "maybe things happen that could have been avoided," such as where there is a choice not to kill, but he would not impose it for killing "in self[-]defense sometimes."  App. 202.2-202.3.  Where the death penalty is sought for murder committed at the same time as another felony, Warren thought that it "depends on the case and the circumstances involved at the time."  <em>Id.</em>, at 204.  None of these responses is exceptionable.  A number of venire members not struck by the State, including some seated on the jury, offered some version of the uncontroversial, and responsible, view that imposition of the death penalty ought to depend on the circumstances.  See Joint Lodging 176 (Marie Mazza, a seated juror); <em>id.</em>, at 223 (Filemon Zablan, a seated juror); App. 548 (Colleen Moses, struck by the defense); <em>id.</em>, at 618 (Mary Witt, struck by the defense); 11-(B) Record 4455-4456 (Max O'Dell, struck by the defense).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;There were other black members of the venire struck purportedly because of some ambivalence, about the death penalty or their capacity to impose it, who Miller-El argues must actually have been struck because of race, none of them having expressed any more ambivalence than white jurors Mazza and Hearn.  We think these are closer calls, however.  Edwin Rand said at points that he could impose the death penalty, but he also said "right now I say I can, but tomorrow I might not."  App. 265 (alterations omitted).  Wayman Kennedy testified that he could impose the death penalty, but on his questionnaire and <em>voir dire</em>, he was more specific, saying that he believed in the death penalty for mass murder. <em> Id.</em>, at 317; Joint Lodging 46.  (Arguably Fernando Gutierrez, accepted by the prosecution, expressed a similar view when he offered as an example of a defendant who merited the death penalty a "criminally insane" person who could not be rehabilitated.  App. 777.  But perhaps prosecutors took Gutierrez to mean this only as an example.)  Roderick Bozeman stated that he thought he could vote for the death penalty but he didn't really know.  <em>Id.,</em> at 145.  Finally, Carrol Boggess expressed uncertainty whether she could go through with giving the death penalty, <em>id.</em>, at 298-299, although she later averred that she could, <em>id.</em>, at 302-304.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;We do not decide whether there were white jurors who expressed ambivalence just as much as these black members of the venire panel.  There is no need to go into these instances, for the prosecutors' treatment of Fields and Warren supports stronger arguments that <em>Batson</em> was violated.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The procedure is conducted under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann., Art. 35.11 (Vernon Supp. 2004-2005).  While that statute says that the court clerk is to conduct a shuffle on the request of either party, the transcripts in this case make clear that each side did its own shuffles.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, App. 124.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Of the first 10 panel members before the prosecution shuffled, 4 were black.  Of the second 10, 3 were black.  Of the third 10, 2 were black, and only 1 black was among the last 10 panel members.  2 Record 837.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNopinion1.14" href="#FRopinion1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court of Appeals declined to give much weight to the evidence of racially motivated jury shuffles because "Miller-El shuffled the jury five times and the prosecutors shuffled the jury only twice."  361 F.&nbsp;3d, at 855.  But Miller-El's shuffles are flatly irrelevant to the question whether prosecutors' shuffles revealed a desire to exclude blacks.  (The Appeals Court's statement was also inaccurate: the prosecution shuffled the jury three times.)</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNopinion1.15" href="#FRopinion1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;So far as we can tell from the voluminous record before us, many of the juror questionnaires, along with juror information cards, were added to the habeas record after the filing of the petition in the District Court.  See Supplemental Briefing on <em>Batson</em>/<em>Swain</em> Claim Based on Previously Unavailable Evidence, Record in No. 00-10784 (CA5), p. 2494.  The State raised no objection to receipt of the supplemental material in the District Court or the Fifth Circuit, and in this Court the State has joined with Miller-El in proposing that we consider this material, by providing additional copies in a joint lodging (apparently as an alternative to a more costly printing as part of the joint appendix).  Neither party has referred to the provision that the reasonableness of the state-court determination be judged by the evidence before the state court, 28 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 2254(d)(2), and it is not clear to what extent the lodged material expands upon what the state judge knew; the same judge presided over the <em>voir dire</em>, the <em>Swain</em> hearing, and the <em>Batson</em> hearing, and the jury questionnaires were subjects of reference at the <em>voir dire</em>.  The last time this case was here the State expressly relied on the questionnaires for one of its arguments, Brief for Respondent in <em>Miller-El</em> v. <em>Cockrell</em>, O.&nbsp;T. 2002, No. 01-7662, p.&nbsp;17, and although it objected to the Court's consideration of some other evidence not before the state courts, <em>id.</em>, at 28-29, it did not object either to questionnaires or juror cards.  This time around, the State again relies on the jury questionnaires for its argument that the prosecution's disparate questioning was not based on race.  We have no occasion here to reach any question about waiver under 2254(d)(2).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is worth noting that if we excluded the lodged material in this case, the State's arguments would fare even worse than they do.  The panel members' cards and answers to the questionnaires were the only items of information that the prosecutors had about them, other than their appearances, before reaching the point of choosing whether to employ the graphic script; if we excluded consideration of the questionnaires, the State would be left with no basis even to argue extenuation of the extreme racial disparity in the use of the graphic script.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNopinion1.16" href="#FRopinion1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;We confine our analysis to these sources because the questionnaires and any testimony about their answers provided the only information available to prosecutors about venire members' views on the death penalty before they decided whether to use the graphic script.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNopinion1.17" href="#FRopinion1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent has conducted a similar statistical analysis that it contends supports the State's argument that the graphic script was used to expose the true feelings of jurors who professed ambivalence about the death penalty on their questionnaires.  See <em>post</em>, at 24-31.  A few examples suffice to show that the dissent's conclusions rest on characterizations of panel members' questionnaire responses that we consider implausible.  In the dissent's analysis, for example, Keaton and Mackey were ambivalent, despite Keaton's questionnaire response that she did not believe in the death penalty and felt it was not for her to punish anyone, Joint Lodging 55, and Mackey's response that "[t]hou shall [n]ot kill," <em>id.</em>, at 79.  But we believe neither can be fairly characterized as someone who might turn out to be a juror acceptable to the State upon pointed questioning.  The dissent also characterizes the questionnaires of Vivian Sztybel, Filemon Zablan, and Dominick Desinise as revealing ambivalence.  But Sztybel's questionnaire stated that she believed in the death penalty "[i]f a person is found guilty of murder or other crime ... without a valid defense" because "[t]hey may continue to do this again and again."  <em>Id.</em>, at 184.  She also reported that she had no moral, religious, or personal belief that would prevent her from imposing the death penalty.  <em>Ibid</em>.  Zablan stated on the questionnaire that he was able to impose the death penalty and that he supported it "[i]f it's the law and if the crime fits such punishment."  <em>Id.</em>, at 223.  Desinise reported in <em>voir dire</em> that he had stated in the questionnaire his opposition to the death penalty.  App. 573.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNopinion1.18" href="#FRopinion1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;App. 728 (Mackey); <em>id.</em>, at 769 (Keaton).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNopinion1.19" href="#FRopinion1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Kennedy said that he believed in the death penalty but would apply it only in an extreme case such as one involving multiple murders.  Joint Lodging 46.  There is no ambivalence in his questionnaire responses.  Butler's questionnaire is not available, but she affirmed in <em>voir dire</em> that she had said on her questionnaire that she believed in the death penalty, that she had no moral, religious, or personal beliefs that would prevent her from imposing the death penalty, and that she had reported on her questionnaire that she "believe[d] in the death penalty only when a crime has been committed concerning a child such as beating to death or some form of harsh physical abuse and when an innocent victim's life is taken."  4 Record 1874; see also <em>id.</em>, at 1906-1907.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNopinion1.20" href="#FRopinion1.20">20</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;App. 579 (Butler); <em>id.</em>, at 317 (Kennedy).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNopinion1.21" href="#FRopinion1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Id.</em>, at 640-641 (Sztybel); <em>id.</em>, at 748 (Zablan).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNopinion1.22" href="#FRopinion1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Joint Lodging 184 (Sztybel); <em>id.,</em> at 223 (Zablan).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNopinion1.23" href="#FRopinion1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Neither questionnaire is available, but Desinise and Evans both confirmed on <em>voir dire</em> that on the questionnaire they stated their opposition to the death penalty.  App. 573 (Desinise), <em>id.</em>, at 626-628 (Evans).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNopinion1.24" href="#FRopinion1.24">24</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Id.,</em> at 573 (Desinise); <em>id.</em>, at 626 (Evans).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNopinion1.25" href="#FRopinion1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In answering the question whether she had moral, religious, or personal beliefs that might prevent her from giving the death penalty, Colleen Moses confirmed at <em>voir dire</em> that she said, "I don't know.  It would depend."  3 Record 1141.  Noad Vickery confirmed at <em>voir dire</em> that he reported on the questionnaire that he was not sure what he believed about the death penalty.  4 <em>id.</em>, at 1611.  Fernando Gutierrez reported on the questionnaire that he believed in the death penalty for some crimes but answered "yes" to the question whether he had moral, religious, or personal beliefs that might prevent him from imposing it.  Joint Lodging 231.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNopinion1.26" href="#FRopinion1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;App. 775 (Gutierrez); <em>id.</em>, at 547 (Moses); 4 Record 1569 (Vickery).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 27" name="FNopinion1.27" href="#FRopinion1.27">27</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;These were Linda Baker, Joint Lodging 71; Paul Bailey, <em>id.</em>, at 63; Carrol Boggess, <em>id.</em>, at 38; and Troy Woods, <em>id.</em>, at 207.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 28" name="FNopinion1.28" href="#FRopinion1.28">28</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;App. 294 (Boggess); <em>id.</em>, at 652-653 (Baker); <em>id.</em>, at 405-406 (Woods), <em>id.</em>, at 737 (Bailey).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 29" name="FNopinion1.29" href="#FRopinion1.29">29</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent posits that prosecutors did not use the graphic script with panel members opposed to the death penalty because it would only have antagonized them.  See <em>post</em>, at 29.  No answer is offered to the question why a prosecutor would take care with the feelings of a panel member he would excuse for cause or strike yet would antagonize an ambivalent member whose feelings he wanted to smoke out, but who might turn out to be an acceptable juror.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 30" name="FNopinion1.30" href="#FRopinion1.30">30</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;These were John Nelson, 2 Record 625; James Holtz, <em>id.</em>, at 1022; Moses, 3 <em>id.</em>, at 1141; Linda Berk, <em>id.</em>, at 1445, 1450; Desinise, App. 573; Vickery, 4 Record 1610; Gene Hinson,<em> </em>App. 576; Girard, <em>id.</em>, at 624; Evans, <em>id.</em>, at 627-628; Gutierrez, Joint Lodging 231.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 31" name="FNopinion1.31" href="#FRopinion1.31">31</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;These were Desinise, App. 573; Evans, <em>id.</em>, at 626; and Gutierrez, <em>id.</em>, at 775.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 32" name="FNopinion1.32" href="#FRopinion1.32">32</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;These were Jerry Mosley, 7 Record 2658; Baker, <em>id.</em>, at 71; Bailey, <em>id.</em>, at 63; Keaton, <em>id.</em>, at 55; Mackey, <em>id.</em>, at 79; Boggess, <em>id.</em>, at 38; and Woods, <em>id.</em>, at 207.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 33" name="FNopinion1.33" href="#FRopinion1.33">33</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Only Mosley did not.  App. 630.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 34" name="FNopinion1.34" href="#FRopinion1.34">34</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The State puts the number of black panel members who expressed opposition or ambivalence at seven, and each received the minimum punishment ruse.  Bozeman, <em>id.,</em> at 162; Fields, <em>id.</em>, at 187-188; Warren, <em>id.</em>, at 213-214; Rand, <em>id.</em>, at 270; Boggess, <em>id.</em>, at 306-307; Kennedy, <em>id.</em>, at 327-328; and Baker, <em>id.</em>, at 654.  Woods, the State argues, had been revealed through questioning as a supporter of the death penalty, and accordingly he was told that five years was the statutory minimum.  As explained <em>supra</em>, at 7-18, Fields and Warren were neither ambivalent nor opposed; on our analysis of black venire members opposed or ambivalent, all received the trick question, along with two proponents of capital punishment.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 35" name="FNopinion1.35" href="#FRopinion1.35">35</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Moses confirmed at <em>voir dire</em> that she reported on her questionnaire that she did not know the answer to Question 58, 3 Record 1141, although she did express support for the death penalty, App. 548.  She was not subjected to the manipulative script.  <em>Id.</em>, at 547.  Crowson said that if there was a chance at rehabilitation she probably would not go with death.  <em>Id.</em>, at 554.  The prosecution used a peremptory strike against her but did not employ the manipulative minimum punishment script.  3 Record 1232.  Vickery said he did not know how he felt about the death penalty, 4 <em>id.</em>, at 1572, but was not subjected to the manipulative script, <em>id.</em>, at 1582.  Salsini thought he would have a problem in the future if he voted to impose a death sentence, App. 593, but he was not subjected to the script, <em>id.</em>, at 595.  Mazza was worried about what other people would think if she imposed the death penalty, <em>id.,</em> at 354-355, but was not subjected to the script, <em>id.</em>, at 356.  Witt said she did not know if she could give the death penalty, 6 Record 2423, but was not subjected to the script, <em>id.</em>, at 2439.  Whaley thought that she could not give the death penalty without proof of premeditation, even though Texas law did not require it, 10 <em>id.</em>, at 3750, but she was not subjected to the script, <em>id.</em>, at 3768.  Hearn said that the death penalty should be given only to those who could not be rehabilitated, App. 429, but she was not subjected to the script, <em>id.</em>, at 441.  The three nonblacks who expressed ambivalence or opposition and were subjected to the script were James Holtz, <em>id.</em>, at 538; Margaret Gibson, <em>id</em>., at 514; and Fernando Gutierrez, 11-(B) Record 4397.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 36" name="FNopinion1.36" href="#FRopinion1.36">36</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent reaches a different statistical result that supports the State's explanation.  See <em>post</em>, at 31-33.  There are two flaws in its calculations.  First, it excises from its calculations panel members who were struck for cause or by agreement, on the theory that prosecutors knew they could be rid of those panel members without resorting to the minimum punishment ruse.  See <em>post</em>, at 31-32.  But the prosecution's calculation about whether to ask these manipulative questions occurred before prosecutors asked the trial court to strike panel members for cause and, frequently, before prosecutors and defense counsel would have reached agreement about removal.  It is unlikely that prosecutors were so assured of being able to remove certain panel members for cause or by agreement that they would forgo the chance to create additional grounds for removal by employing the minimum-punishment ruse.  Second, as with its analysis of the panelists receiving the graphic script, the dissent characterizes certain panel members in ways that in our judgment are unconvincing.  For example, for purposes of the minimum-punishment analysis, the dissent considers Colleen Moses and Noad Vickery to be panelists so favorable to the prosecution that there was no need to resort to the minimum-punishment ruse, <em>post</em>, at 32, yet the dissent acknowledged Moses's and Vickery's ambivalent questionnaire responses in its discussion of the graphic script, <em>post</em>, at 29.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 37" name="FNopinion1.37" href="#FRopinion1.37">37</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The material omitted from the quotation includes an excerpt from a 1963 circular given to prosecutors in the District Attorney's Office, which the State points out was not in evidence in the state trial court.  The Sparling Manual, however, was before the state court.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 38" name="FNopinion1.38" href="#FRopinion1.38">38</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The State claimed at oral argument that prosecutors could have been tracking jurors' races to be sure of avoiding a <em>Batson</em> violation.  Tr. of Oral Arg. 44.  <em>Batson</em>, of course, was decided the month after Miller-El was tried.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNdissent1.1" href="#FRdissent1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The supplemental material appears in a joint lodging submitted by the parties.  It includes the State's copies of questionnaires for 12 prospective jurors (11 of whom served at Miller-El's trial), and the State's juror cards for all 108 members of the venire panel.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNdissent1.2" href="#FRdissent1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The juror questionnaires had been in Miller-El's possession since before the 1986 <em>Swain</em> hearing; Miller-El's attorneys used them during the <em>voir dire</em>.  But because Miller-El did not argue disparate treatment or questioning at the <em>Batson</em> hearing, Miller-El's attorneys had no reason to submit the questionnaires to the trial court.  The juror cards could have been requested at any point under the Texas Public Information Act.  See Supplemental Briefing on <em>Batson</em>/<em>Swain</em> Claim Based on Previously Unavailable Evidence, Record in No. 00-10784 (CA5), p. 2494.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNdissent1.3" href="#FRdissent1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority argues that prosecutors mischaracterized Fields' testimony when they struck him.  <em>Ante</em>, at 10.  This is partially true but wholly irrelevant.  When Miller-El's counsel suggested that Fields' strike was related to race, prosecutor Jim Nelson responded:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"[W]e're certainly not exercising a preemptory strike on Mr. Fields because of his race in this case, but we do have concern with reference to some of his statements as to the death penalty in that he said that he could only give death if he thought a person could not be rehabilitated and he later made the comment that any person could be rehabilitated if they find God or are introduced to God and the fact that we have a concern that his religious feelings may affect his jury service in this case."  App. 197 (alteration omitted).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nelson partially misstated Fields' testimony.  Fields had not said that he would give the death penalty only if a person was beyond rehabilitation, <em>id.</em>, at 185, but he had said that any person could be rehabilitated if introduced to God, <em>id.</em>, at 184.  This is precisely why prosecutors were concerned that Fields' "religious feelings [might] affect his jury service."  <em>Id.</em>, at 197.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNdissent1.4" href="#FRdissent1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In explaining why veniremen Hearn, Witt, and Gutierrez were more favorable to the State than Fields, the majority faults me for "focus[ing] on reasons the prosecution itself did not offer."  <em>Ante</em>, at 11, n.&nbsp;4.  The majority's complaint is hard to understand.  The State <em>accepted</em> Hearn, Witt, and Gutierrez.  Although it is apparent from the <em>voir dire</em> transcript why the State wanted to seat these veniremen on the jury, it was never required to "offer" its reasons for doing so.  If the majority instead means that I focus on whether these veniremen opposed the death penalty and whether they had relatives with significant criminal histories, those are precisely the reasons offered by the State for its strike of Fields.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNdissent1.5" href="#FRdissent1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority's own recitation of the <em>voir dire</em> transcript captures Butler's ambivalence.  <em>Ante</em>, at 25, n.&nbsp;19.  Butler said both that she had no qualms about imposing the death penalty, 4 Record 1906-1907, and that she would impose the death penalty "only when a crime has been committed concerning a child such as beating to death or some form of harsh physical abuse and when an innocent victim's life is taken," <em>id.</em>, at 1874.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNdissent1.6" href="#FRdissent1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The State's concerns with Fields and Warren stemmed not from their questionnaire responses, but from their subsequent <em>voir dire</em> testimony.  <em>Supra</em>, at 15-16, 20-21.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNdissent1.7" href="#FRdissent1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Joint Lodging 184 (Sztybel) ("If a person is found guilty of murder or other crime, which they have taken someone else's life, without a valid defense.  They may continue to do this again and again.  Even if they are sentenced to jail when they are released this could keep happening"); <em>id.</em>, at 223 (Zablan) ("If it's the law and if the crime fits such punishment").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNdissent1.8" href="#FRdissent1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Girard did not answer question 56 about her belief in the death penalty, 6 Record 2522, but she indicated in answer to question 58 that her personal beliefs would not prevent her from imposing the death penalty, <em>id.</em>, at 2555-2556.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNdissent1.9" href="#FRdissent1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In making the decision whether to employ the MPS, prosecutors could rely on both the questionnaires and substantial <em>voir dire</em> testimony, because the minimum punishment questioning occurred much later in the <em>voir dire</em> than questioning about the death penalty.  <em>Miller-El I</em>, 537 U.&nbsp;S. 322, 369 (2003) (<em>Thomas, J.</em>, dissenting).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNdissent1.10" href="#FRdissent1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Moses gave ambivalent answers on her questionnaire, as perhaps did Vickery.  <em>Supra</em>, at 29.  However, Moses and Vickery indicated during their <em>voir dire</em> testimony that they could impose the death penalty, 3 Record 1139-1141; 4 <em>id.</em>, at 1576-1579, and thus they were not questioned on minimum sentences.  But see <em>ante</em>, at 30, n. 36.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNdissent1.11" href="#FRdissent1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Judge Larry Baraka, one of the first black prosecutors to serve in the D.&nbsp;A.'s Office, testified that, to the best of his recollection, the Manual was no longer used in 1977 when he attended the training course.  App. 844.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

    <div class="lawyer-search-section__search">
        
    <div class="lawyer-search">
    <h2 class="lawyer-search__search-title">Need to find an attorney?</h2>
    <p class="lawyer-search__search-subtitle">Search our directory by legal issue</p>
    <p class="lawyer-search__form-instructions">Enter information in one or both fields (Required)</p>
    <form class="lawyer-search__form" method="get" action="https://lawyers.findlaw.com/lawyer/lawyer_dir/search/jsp/stdSearch_process.jsp">
        <div class="lawyer-search__input-group lawyer-search__input-group--legal-issue">
            <label class="lawyer-search__input-label" for="lawyer-search__input--legal-issue-1">Legal issue</label>
            <input class="lawyer-search__input" name="keyword" type="search" data-title=".search-title" placeholder="" maxlength="255" autocomplete="off" aria-autocomplete="both" aria-haspopup="listbox" aria-expanded="false" role="combobox" id="lawyer-search__input--legal-issue-1" data-target="lawyer-search__autocomplete--legal-issue-1" aria-controls="lawyer-search__autocomplete--legal-issue-1">
            <button class="lawyer-search__input-clear" type="button" aria-label="Clear input">
                <i class="fa fa-times-circle" aria-hidden="true"></i>
            </button>

    <script src="//caselaw.findlaw.com/static/fi/images/etc/designs/flcommon/flfe/javascript/lp.redesign.js"></script><script>_satellite["_runScript1"](function(event, target, Promise) {
_satellite.ads = {};

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

_satellite.ads.sendConversionFindLaw = function() {
  var subId = _satellite.getVar('current_ad_eng').sub;
  var cookieName = 'fl_ad_'+subId;
  var cookieValue = 'true';

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

window.addEventListener("load", function(event) {
  try {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {
      const forms = document.querySelectorAll("form[action*='stdSearch_process.jsp']");
      _satellite.logger.info('forms',forms);
      forms.forEach((form) => {
        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA
    const links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');
    _satellite.logger.info('links',links);
    links.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {

          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {

            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
